## Chapter 10 — Add Essential Policies

This chapter is decision and review work: you decide and record your store policies using Shopify’s tools. It is not legal advice, and policy text can be edited later — but the words become customer-facing, so review them before publishing.

### What you will complete

By the end of this chapter, you will have a list of the policies that apply to your current store; a written decision for each required policy topic; reviewed return/refund, shipping/delivery, privacy, and terms-of-service drafts; reviewed public contact information; identified subscription, legal-notice, or special-product policies where applicable; your written policies compared against your actual fulfillment and store operations; policy links added to appropriate menus; checkout and storefront policy links opened and reviewed; a record of unresolved legal, operational, and factual blockers; and a clear decision on whether the store is ready for Chapter 11 testing. This chapter does not run test orders, perform a full legal-compliance review, guarantee enforceability, or launch the store.

### Why this matters

Policies tell customers how your store handles returns, shipping, privacy, and the terms of a sale — and they should describe how your store *actually* operates. Working through them helps the merchant turn real business facts into clear, consistent public wording and helps identify gaps that require a decision or professional review. Policies that do not match operations, or that were published without review, are a common source of avoidable confusion.

### Legal and professional-review boundary

This chapter is a policy-planning and Shopify-configuration workflow. It is **NOT legal advice**, and nothing in it decides which laws apply to your business. Shopify-generated policy templates and automated privacy tools are starting points, not substitutes for legal advice; publishing a policy does not by itself establish compliance or protect the business from disputes, refunds, chargebacks, complaints, or liability. Your policies should reflect your actual products, fulfillment methods, locations, third-party services, and customer-contact process, and the requirements that apply where you and your customers are located. Where an exclusion, regional requirement, liability clause, dispute or governing-law provision, subscription rule, or data practice is uncertain, treat it as a blocker and seek qualified legal review. Written policies and operational settings (such as Shopify return rules) can be separate systems that require a consistency review.

### Step-by-step instructions

1. **Review the business facts before drafting policies.** Return to Chapters 1, 2, 5, 7, 8, and 9 and record: the business identity used publicly; the public contact method; products sold; which are physical, POD, handmade, digital, service, and commission products; the fulfillment party; fulfillment locations; the expected processing workflow; delivery methods; return destinations; current return and cancellation capabilities; third-party providers and apps; countries or regions served; customer information collected; marketing and analytics tools; and subscription products, if any. Do not draft policy terms around facts that are unknown. *Why it matters:* this helps identify which facts each policy must accurately reflect. *Done when:* you have a factual business-operation summary and a blocker list.

2. **Identify which policy areas apply.** Review return and refund policy, shipping and delivery policy, privacy policy, terms of service, public contact information, subscription or cancellation policy (if applicable), legal notice (if applicable), commission or personalized-product terms, digital-product terms, service terms, and product-specific safety or use notices where genuinely required. Not every store needs every optional policy type in identical form. *Why it matters:* this identifies which policy areas require a decision for the current business. *Done when:* every policy area is marked Required, Applicable, Not currently applicable, or Needs professional review.

3. **Locate Shopify's policy area.** The current path is **Settings → Policies**, but this path is change-sensitive. *Why it matters:* this helps locate the area where Shopify stores written policy content. *Done when:* you can locate the current written-policy fields.
   [Shopify — Add store policies](https://help.shopify.com/en/manual/checkout-settings/refund-privacy-tos) · `[VERIFY CURRENT SHOPIFY SETTING]`
   `[INSERT SCREENSHOT — fictional Shopify written-policy overview with all sensitive information fully redacted]` *(Locate: the written-policy overview and the available policy fields or destinations. Hide: every visible value that could identify the business, a person, a policy decision, or a private operation. All business names, personal names, addresses, emails, phone numbers, domains, URLs, policy text, customer information, order information, payment information, tax information, legal identifiers, app identifiers, analytics identifiers, regions, provider information, return locations, and private operational details must be fictional, blank, or fully redacted. Capture date: record the screenshot capture date. Note: Shopify paths, policy labels, templates, return rules, automated privacy behavior, cookie controls, menus, checkout links, theme display, and regional controls can change.)*

4. **Decide whether to start from a template, automated tool, or original draft.** A Shopify template can provide a starting structure; automated privacy settings can populate or update certain privacy content; and original text can be entered where supported. A generated template is not automatically complete or suitable; automated text can contain placeholders or assumptions requiring review; language availability can vary; and professional review can be appropriate when the business has unusual products, international customers, subscriptions, regulated goods, high-value items, complex commissions, or unresolved legal questions. This guide does not recommend one route universally. *Why it matters:* this records how each policy draft will be created and reviewed. *Done when:* each policy has a documented drafting source and reviewer.

5. **Create a policy fact register.** Before writing policy language, record the actual answers to: what products can be returned; what products cannot be returned (if legally and operationally supportable); where a return would be sent; who pays return shipping; how damaged, defective, incorrect, or missing items are handled; whether an order can be cancelled; when production begins; how commissions are approved; how digital products are delivered; what customer information is collected; which third parties process information; how customers can contact the business; what processing and delivery information can be stated accurately; whether customs charges or import fees are possible; whether subscriptions are offered; and whether age, safety, or use restrictions apply. This guide does not answer these questions for you. *Why it matters:* this helps separate factual business decisions from generated policy language. *Done when:* unanswered items are recorded as blockers rather than invented.

6. **Draft and review the return/refund policy.** Decide and document — without using universal terms supplied by this guide — return eligibility, the return-request procedure, the return destination, product-condition requirements, timeframes you choose and review against applicable requirements, return-shipping responsibility, original-shipping treatment, refund method, refund-processing workflow, exchanges (if offered), handling of damaged/defective/incorrect/missing items, final-sale or non-returnable categories where supportable, and specific treatment for POD, handmade and one-of-one, personalized and commissioned, digital, and service products, plus gift cards, sale items, and cancellation before production or fulfillment, and the required customer-contact information. Do not invent a return window; do not state that personalized, digital, handmade, sale, or POD products are automatically non-returnable; applicable laws and platform obligations can override a merchant's preferred terms; and obtain legal guidance for uncertain exclusions or regional requirements. *Why it matters:* this helps document how return, refund, damage, and cancellation requests are intended to be handled. *Done when:* the written policy matches current operational decisions or unresolved issues are recorded.
   [Shopify — Write a return policy](https://help.shopify.com/en/manual/checkout-settings/refund-privacy-tos) · [Shopify — Consumer-protection guidance](https://help.shopify.com/en/manual/compliance/legal/consumer-protection) · `[VERIFY CURRENT SHOPIFY SETTING]`
   `[INSERT SCREENSHOT — fictional return/refund policy editor with all sensitive information fully redacted]` *(Locate: the return/refund policy editor and the field where the reviewed draft is entered. Hide: every visible value that could identify the business, a person, a policy decision, or a private operation. All business names, personal names, addresses, emails, phone numbers, domains, URLs, policy text, customer information, order information, payment information, tax information, legal identifiers, app identifiers, analytics identifiers, regions, provider information, return locations, and private operational details must be fictional, blank, or fully redacted. Capture date: record the screenshot capture date. Note: Shopify paths, policy labels, templates, return rules, automated privacy behavior, cookie controls, menus, checkout links, theme display, and regional controls can change.)*

7. **Compare the written return policy with Shopify return rules.** Written policy text and operational return rules are separate. Review return-window settings (if used), return-shipping-fee settings, restocking-fee settings, final-sale product rules, customer self-serve return behavior, staff return workflow, and app-managed return behavior. Do not configure detailed returns infrastructure beyond what is required to identify inconsistency. Changing return rules does not necessarily update the written return/refund policy, and editing the written policy does not necessarily update operational return rules. *Why it matters:* this helps identify differences between public policy wording and operational return settings. *Done when:* any mismatch is corrected or recorded as a blocker.
   [Shopify — Set up return rules](https://help.shopify.com/en/manual/orders/refunds-returns/return-rules) · `[VERIFY CURRENT SHOPIFY SETTING]`
   `[INSERT SCREENSHOT — fictional return-rules settings with all sensitive information fully redacted]` *(Locate: the operational return-rules settings used to compare return windows, fees, final-sale rules, or related return behavior with the written policy. Hide: every visible value that could identify the business, a person, a policy decision, or a private operation. All business names, personal names, addresses, emails, phone numbers, domains, URLs, policy text, customer information, order information, payment information, tax information, legal identifiers, app identifiers, analytics identifiers, regions, provider information, return locations, and private operational details must be fictional, blank, or fully redacted. Capture date: record the screenshot capture date. Note: Shopify paths, policy labels, templates, return rules, automated privacy behavior, cookie controls, menus, checkout links, theme display, and regional controls can change.)*

8. **Draft and review the shipping and delivery policy.** Decide about processing time versus transit time; self-fulfilled, POD, handmade, made-to-order, commission, and digital/service products; multiple fulfillment sources and split shipments; tracking availability; delivery estimates; address errors; carrier delays; lost, damaged, or marked-delivered packages; international shipping; customs duties, import taxes, or fees; geographic restrictions; PO boxes or special addresses where applicable; and customer contact during delays. Do not invent shipping times, carrier guarantees, refund promises, customs outcomes, or provider performance. Third-party fulfillment should be disclosed where relevant, and the policy should match Chapter 7. *Why it matters:* this records what customers are told about processing, delivery, third-party fulfillment, and shipping exceptions. *Done when:* shipping wording matches the actual fulfillment paths or blockers are recorded.
   [Shopify — Write a shipping policy](https://help.shopify.com/en/manual/checkout-settings/refund-privacy-tos) · `[VERIFY CURRENT SHOPIFY SETTING]`
   `[INSERT SCREENSHOT — fictional shipping-policy editor with all sensitive information fully redacted]` *(Locate: the shipping-policy editor and the field where processing, delivery, and fulfillment wording is reviewed. Hide: every visible value that could identify the business, a person, a policy decision, or a private operation. All business names, personal names, addresses, emails, phone numbers, domains, URLs, policy text, customer information, order information, payment information, tax information, legal identifiers, app identifiers, analytics identifiers, regions, provider information, return locations, and private operational details must be fictional, blank, or fully redacted. Capture date: record the screenshot capture date. Note: Shopify paths, policy labels, templates, return rules, automated privacy behavior, cookie controls, menus, checkout links, theme display, and regional controls can change.)*

9. **Review processing and delivery claims across the store.** Compare the shipping policy with product descriptions, announcement bars, FAQ pages, cart text, checkout text, order confirmations, provider estimates, commission instructions, social-media claims, and (later, where relevant) marketplace listings. Do not expand into a full-site editorial project. *Why it matters:* this helps identify conflicting delivery or processing statements. *Done when:* inconsistent claims are recorded for correction.

10. **Review the privacy-policy approach.** A privacy policy describes how the business collects, uses, shares, stores, or otherwise handles personal information. Shopify can currently provide automated privacy settings; automated privacy content can change when certain Shopify settings change; and automated settings do not replace merchant review or legal advice. The policy must reflect apps, pixels, analytics, marketing tools, payment providers, fulfillment partners, customer-service tools, and other actual data practices; should not list tools you do not use; and should not have required disclosures removed merely to shorten it. *Why it matters:* this helps identify whether the privacy policy reflects the store's current data practices. *Done when:* an automated or manually maintained privacy-policy approach is deliberately selected.
    [Shopify — Privacy overview](https://help.shopify.com/en/manual/privacy-and-security/privacy) · [Shopify — Manage customer privacy](https://help.shopify.com/en/manual/privacy-and-security/privacy/customer-privacy-settings) · [Shopify — Configure privacy settings](https://help.shopify.com/en/manual/privacy-and-security/privacy/customer-privacy-settings/privacy-settings) · `[VERIFY CURRENT SHOPIFY SETTING]`

11. **Review automated privacy settings.** Review — without deciding legal requirements — the automated privacy policy, cookie banner, data-sharing opt-out page, the regions where controls display, current data-sharing settings, the Shopify network-intelligence setting, installed privacy apps, marketing-consent settings, translation or localization behavior, and policy history or update records where supported. Do not activate or deactivate privacy controls solely because this guide says to; requirements depend on your operations and customer locations; privacy changes can affect analytics and marketing data collection; and significant uncertainty should be referred for qualified legal review. *Why it matters:* this helps identify which privacy controls are active and which require review. *Done when:* the current privacy configuration and unresolved questions are documented.
    `[VERIFY CURRENT SHOPIFY SETTING]`
    `[INSERT SCREENSHOT — fictional automated privacy-policy settings with all sensitive information fully redacted]` *(Locate: the automated privacy-policy settings and any visible automation or update status. Hide: every visible value that could identify the business, a person, a policy decision, or a private operation. All business names, personal names, addresses, emails, phone numbers, domains, URLs, policy text, customer information, order information, payment information, tax information, legal identifiers, app identifiers, analytics identifiers, regions, provider information, return locations, and private operational details must be fictional, blank, or fully redacted. Hide: every visible value that could identify the business, a person, a policy decision, or a private operation. All business names, personal names, addresses, emails, phone numbers, domains, URLs, policy text, customer information, order information, payment information, tax information, legal identifiers, app identifiers, analytics identifiers, regions, provider information, return locations, and private operational details must be fictional, blank, or fully redacted. Capture date: record the screenshot capture date. Note: Shopify paths, policy labels, templates, return rules, automated privacy behavior, cookie controls, menus, checkout links, theme display, and regional controls can change.)*

12. **Review the terms of service.** Review the store identity, customer eligibility, product/service descriptions, order acceptance, pricing and payment, errors and corrections, account use, prohibited use, intellectual property, third-party services, user submissions, disclaimers, liability language, indemnification language, governing-law or dispute language, changes to terms, and contact information. Do not draft jurisdiction-specific clauses as settled law; template clauses involving liability, disputes, governing law, warranties, and indemnification are strong candidates for professional review. *Why it matters:* this helps identify whether the terms describe the transaction and contain clauses requiring legal review. *Done when:* business-specific facts are accurate and high-risk clauses are flagged.
    [Shopify — Write terms of service](https://help.shopify.com/en/manual/checkout-settings/refund-privacy-tos) · `[VERIFY CURRENT SHOPIFY SETTING]`
    `[INSERT SCREENSHOT — fictional terms-of-service editor with all sensitive information fully redacted]` *(Locate: the terms-of-service editor and the field containing the draft under review. Hide: every visible value that could identify the business, a person, a policy decision, or a private operation. All business names, personal names, addresses, emails, phone numbers, domains, URLs, policy text, customer information, order information, payment information, tax information, legal identifiers, app identifiers, analytics identifiers, regions, provider information, return locations, and private operational details must be fictional, blank, or fully redacted. Capture date: record the screenshot capture date. Note: Shopify paths, policy labels, templates, return rules, automated privacy behavior, cookie controls, menus, checkout links, theme display, and regional controls can change.)*

13. **Review public contact information.** Confirm a public business or support name; a customer-service email or contact method; a mailing or business address where legally and operationally appropriate; a phone number only if deliberately offered; response-process wording without invented response-time guarantees; and consistency across policies, the contact page, checkout, receipts, and business information. Do not expose a residential address without understanding applicable requirements and privacy consequences; use a mailbox, registered-agent, commercial-mail, legal-business, or other address only when lawful and appropriate — this guide does not advise which address is sufficient. *Why it matters:* this helps identify whether customers have a current, deliberate way to contact the business. *Done when:* public contact details are accurate or an address/privacy blocker is recorded.
    `[INSERT SCREENSHOT — fictional public contact-information review with all sensitive information fully redacted]` *(Locate: the public contact fields or page used to compare the support name, contact method, and deliberately published address information. Hide: every visible value that could identify the business, a person, a policy decision, or a private operation. All business names, personal names, addresses, emails, phone numbers, domains, URLs, policy text, customer information, order information, payment information, tax information, legal identifiers, app identifiers, analytics identifiers, regions, provider information, return locations, and private operational details must be fictional, blank, or fully redacted. Capture date: record the screenshot capture date. Note: Shopify paths, policy labels, templates, return rules, automated privacy behavior, cookie controls, menus, checkout links, theme display, and regional controls can change.)*

14. **Review commissions and personalized-product terms.** Cover the information the customer must provide, the approval process, revision limits only if actually chosen, production start, the cancellation point, customer-supplied content, intellectual-property permission, prohibited content, variation in handmade work, digital proof versus physical output, delivery method, handling of damaged or incorrect work, refund or remake handling, unclaimed approvals, and customer-response delays. Do not invent deposits, revision counts, cancellation fees, turnaround times, or ownership terms. *Why it matters:* this helps document how personalized and commissioned orders differ from standard products. *Done when:* commission-specific decisions are documented or blocked.

15. **Review POD and third-party fulfillment disclosures.** Require consistency among product descriptions, the shipping policy, the return policy, the terms, provider-managed production, split shipments, production locations, packaging and branding, address changes, reprints or production errors, and provider return limitations. A provider's policy is not automatically the merchant's customer-facing policy; do not publish terms you cannot operationally follow; Printify remains one fulfillment route, not the universal route; and RJ Print remains separate from Printify. *Why it matters:* this helps identify where third-party fulfillment affects customer-facing terms. *Done when:* provider operations and merchant promises do not visibly conflict, or blockers are recorded.

16. **Review digital products and services, if applicable.** Where applicable, cover the delivery method, access requirements, file format, customer-supplied information, scheduling, rescheduling or cancellation, completion criteria, license or permitted use, support boundaries, refund handling, failed delivery, and third-party platform requirements. Do not invent terms. *Why it matters:* this identifies policy topics that differ from physical-product fulfillment. *Done when:* digital or service-specific terms are documented or marked not applicable.

17. **Review subscription policy requirements, if applicable.** Subscription products can require additional cancellation, renewal, billing, and purchase-option disclosures; Shopify or a subscription app can expose separate subscription-policy behavior; do not add a subscription policy when subscriptions are not offered merely to appear complete; and app terms and storefront terms should be compared. *Why it matters:* this identifies additional terms required by recurring-purchase workflows. *Done when:* subscription policy is completed, marked not applicable, or referred for review.
    [Shopify — Write a subscription policy](https://help.shopify.com/en/manual/checkout-settings/refund-privacy-tos) · `[VERIFY CURRENT SHOPIFY SETTING]`

18. **Review the generated text line by line.** For every policy, check for placeholder brackets, an incorrect business name, an incorrect email, an incorrect address, an incorrect domain, products not sold, services not offered, apps not installed, payment methods not used, regions not served, return terms the business cannot follow, shipping statements that conflict with Chapter 7, subscription language when no subscriptions exist, legal provisions requiring review, internal notes, and template instructions left visible. *Why it matters:* this helps identify template assumptions and incorrect business facts. *Done when:* no known placeholder or false business fact remains.

19. **Compare policies with one another.** Check for conflicts involving return eligibility, cancellation, processing times, delivery times, contact information, business identity, product types, third-party fulfillment, digital products, commissions, subscriptions, governing terms, and last-updated dates. *Why it matters:* this helps identify contradictory terms across separate policy documents. *Done when:* conflicts are corrected or recorded for review.

20. **Save policies deliberately.** Saving can publish or update policy content. Before saving, confirm the correct store, confirm the correct policy field, remove private notes, remove real customer or order information, confirm contact details, confirm the text has been reviewed, record the date, and preserve a copy where appropriate. *Why it matters:* this records the reviewed policy version in the intended field. *Done when:* the intended policy version is saved and its review date recorded.
    `[VERIFY CURRENT SHOPIFY SETTING]`

21. **Add policy links to the footer menu.** Returning to Chapter 9 (the menu area is change-sensitive), review links for the return/refund policy, shipping policy, privacy policy, terms of service, subscription policy (if applicable), legal notice (if applicable), and contact page. Do not create fake or unfinished policy links that appear complete. *Why it matters:* this provides storefront navigation to published policy destinations. *Done when:* applicable published policies are deliberately linked or a blocker is recorded.
    [Shopify — Link policies in menus](https://help.shopify.com/en/manual/online-store/menus-and-links) · `[VERIFY CURRENT SHOPIFY SETTING]`

All business names, personal names, addresses, emails, phone numbers, domains, URLs, policy text, customer information, order information, payment information, tax information, legal identifiers, app identifiers, analytics identifiers, regions, provider information, return locations, and private operational details must be fictional, blank, or fully redacted. 22. **Review automatic checkout and storefront policy links.** Open and review policy links appearing in the checkout footer, order-review area, product pages where applicable, cart where applicable, store footer, and contact or FAQ pages where linked. Not every theme or checkout configuration displays every link identically. *Why it matters:* this helps identify where policy links currently appear and whether they open the intended destination. *Done when:* each visible policy link has been opened and reviewed.

23. **Review policies on desktop and mobile.** Check readability, headings, paragraph spacing, bullets, links, contact details, long unbroken sections, placeholder text, internal notes, incorrect theme styling, the policy destination, and back navigation. Do not redesign the theme. *Why it matters:* this helps identify presentation or access issues on different devices. *Done when:* desktop and mobile issues are recorded.

    `[INSERT SCREENSHOT — fictional mobile policy page with all sensitive information fully redacted]` *(Locate: the same type of published policy page in a mobile viewport, including its heading, body layout, links, and navigation context. Hide: every visible value that could identify the business, a person, a policy decision, or a private operation. All business names, personal names, addresses, emails, phone numbers, domains, URLs, policy text, customer information, order information, payment information, tax information, legal identifiers, app identifiers, analytics identifiers, regions, provider information, return locations, and private operational details must be fictional, blank, or fully redacted. Capture date: record the screenshot capture date. Note: Shopify paths, policy labels, templates, return rules, automated privacy behavior, cookie controls, menus, checkout links, theme display, and regional controls can change.)*

24. **Record legal and operational blockers.** Examples include an unknown return address, an undecided return window, an unclear cancellation rule, an unknown processing time, conflicting provider terms, international-sales uncertainty, privacy-law uncertainty, cookie-banner uncertainty, data-sharing-setting uncertainty, subscription terms, commission-ownership terms, a home-address privacy concern, a regulated product, an age-restricted product, an accessibility concern, an unreviewed liability clause, a governing-law clause, policy translation, and missing contact information. *Why it matters:* this identifies questions that should remain unresolved rather than being filled with invented terms. *Done when:* every blocker has an owner, next action, or professional-review referral.

25. **Make the policy-readiness decision.** Answer: Do the written policies reflect actual store operations? Are public contact details accurate? Do product descriptions and shipping terms agree? Do return rules and written return terms agree? Does the privacy policy reflect actual tools and data practices? Are special product types covered? Are applicable policies linked? Were desktop and mobile links reviewed? Are legal questions clearly separated from operational questions? Are unresolved blockers recorded? *Why it matters:* this informs whether the store is ready for the full customer-journey test in Chapter 11. *Done when:* you can state Ready for Chapter 11, Not ready, or Ready with documented blockers.

### Real example: Lucid Creations

Lucid Creations is the Shopify product-store branch under Auralis Digital. It includes POD products, handmade products, and commissions. POD products can involve Printify or another third-party production route; handmade products include wire wraps first, stone/crystal pendants, pinecone pendants, resin pieces, and related artwork; and RJ Print remains a separate 3D-printing branch. Its policies must distinguish the POD, handmade, commission, and RJ Print workflows. Do not invent Lucid Creations' return window, refund terms, processing time, delivery time, address, provider-error policy, commission cancellation terms, or privacy practices; do not expose a residential address; do not assume Shopify's current policies are correct without reviewing the live store; do not describe Printify's policy as automatically being Lucid Creations' customer policy; and do not make crystal-healing, medical, legal, delivery, quality, refund, or performance guarantees.

`[INSERT LUCID CREATIONS POLICY EXAMPLE]` — current published policies; current Shopify-generated text; current business/contact identity; public support email; an appropriate public return address; current return and cancellation decisions; current shipping and processing facts; the POD-provider, handmade, and commission workflows; the damaged/incorrect-item process; privacy tools and apps; cookie-banner status; data-sharing settings; analytics and marketing tools; current footer and checkout links; any customer confusion; any legal-review questions; and information requiring screenshot redaction.

### Common mistake

Publishing a generated template without checking whether it describes the actual business. This can leave placeholder text, incorrect addresses, products or services not offered, return promises the merchant cannot follow, shipping statements that conflict with fulfillment, missing third-party-production disclosures, privacy disclosures that omit installed apps, subscription language when subscriptions are not used, conflicting policies, and unfinished policy links.

**Instead:** use the template as a starting point, replace assumptions with verified business facts, compare it against operations, and refer unresolved legal questions for qualified review.

### Reality check

A policy is not complete merely because text was generated or saved. The published wording, actual store settings, fulfillment workflow, customer-contact process, and business practices should describe the same operation.

### Checklist

- [ ] I recorded the business facts each policy must reflect.
- [ ] I identified which policy areas apply.
- [ ] I chose a drafting source for each policy.
- [ ] I did not treat generated text as legal advice.
- [ ] My return/refund policy reflects current decisions.
- [ ] I compared written return terms with operational return rules.
- [ ] My shipping policy reflects Chapter 7.
- [ ] I did not invent processing or delivery times.
- [ ] My privacy-policy approach reflects current tools and practices.
- [ ] I reviewed automated privacy settings.
- [ ] I reviewed cookie-banner and data-sharing controls where applicable.
- [ ] I reviewed the terms of service.
- [ ] Public contact information is accurate or blocked for review.
- [ ] Commission terms are addressed where applicable.
- [ ] POD and third-party fulfillment are described accurately.
- [ ] RJ Print remains separate.
- [ ] Digital/service terms are addressed where applicable.
- [ ] Subscription terms are addressed only if applicable.
- [ ] I removed placeholders and incorrect template facts.
- [ ] I compared the policies with one another.
- [ ] I recorded the policy review date.
- [ ] Applicable policy links are in the intended menu.
- [ ] Checkout and storefront links were opened.
- [ ] Desktop and mobile policy pages were reviewed.
- [ ] Legal and operational blockers are recorded.
- [ ] Full customer-journey testing remains deferred to Chapter 11.
- [ ] Launch remains deferred to Chapter 12.

### Before you continue

- [ ] Did I complete this step?
- [ ] Do the policies describe how the store actually operates?
- [ ] Are unresolved legal or operational questions clearly recorded?

<!-- PAGE BREAK -->

## Chapter 11 — Test the Full Customer Journey

This chapter is testing. Use an official simulated test method whenever possible so no real money moves. A deliberately authorized real-payment test is separate, optional, can incur fees, and must not be treated as safe to repeat.

### What you will complete

By the end of this chapter, you will have a written test plan; a safe payment-test method; a pre-test settings record; evidence from at least one complete storefront-to-order path where technically and operationally safe; successful and failed or incomplete scenarios where supported; reviews of navigation, product, variants, cart, checkout, delivery, tax presentation, policies, notifications, inventory, order records, fulfillment routing, desktop, mobile, and accessibility-oriented interaction; restored settings; safely handled test artifacts; a prioritized blocker register; and a decision about whether to proceed to Chapter 12. This chapter does not launch the store.

### Why this matters

Testing helps identify behavior under the scenarios tested. A successful order provides evidence for that path only; another product, location, device, payment method, app, customer state, or fulfillment route could behave differently. Testing does not confirm that the store is error-free, legally compliant, universally tax-correct, or ready to launch by itself.

### Test-safety boundary

Before enabling test mode, placing an order, changing automation, or processing fulfillment, determine whether the store is already accepting orders; whether live customers could be blocked; whether an app can submit, fulfill, email, print, purchase labels, deliver files, or send work to a provider; and whether inventory, discounts, analytics, customer records, or notifications will change. Use controlled test data, prevent real charges and fulfillment unless a real-transaction test is deliberately authorized, record every temporary change, and restore it afterward. Never purchase a shipping label, trigger Printify or RJ Print production, send uninvolved people messages, or expose live credentials or private order links.

**Diagram: Simulated test mode versus live mode**

![Two columns. Simulated test mode: no real payment, prevent real production, inventory or notifications may change, repeat only with safeguards. Live mode: real payment and fees, inventory can change, notifications or fulfillment can trigger, not safe to repeat casually. A note says a deliberately authorized real-payment test is separate, uses live mode, can incur fees, and must not be treated as part of the safe-to-repeat path.](assets/diagrams/test-mode-vs-live.png)

*An official simulated payment does not move real money, but inventory, notifications, and automation can still change. Prevent real production and use controlled recipients. Live mode can cause real charges and downstream actions.*

### Step-by-step instructions

1. **Define the test scope.** List physical, POD, handmade, made-to-order, commission, digital, service, subscription, RJ Print, domestic, international, delivery, pickup, discount, guest, account, and other paths. Mark each Applicable now, Not applicable, Requires separate test, or Blocked. *Why it matters:* this identifies the journeys that exist. *Done when:* the path inventory is written.

2. **Define pass, blocker, and observation.** A Pass matches the documented expectation for this scenario; a Blocker is a material pre-launch issue; an Observation is nonblocking. A pass does not mean compliant, universally correct, or error-free. *Why it matters:* consistent labels make results actionable. *Done when:* each result can be classified.

3. **Create the test-data plan.** Use a controlled inbox for notification delivery; use `@example.com` only when delivery is irrelevant. Use an authorized, scenario-appropriate address for destination-dependent behavior, never another person's address, and redact it from published materials. Record test product, variant, quantity, destination, discount, payment route, label, and cleanup method. Never use real customer records or real card data in a simulated gateway. *Why it matters:* this separates testing from live customer activity. *Done when:* all test data and cleanup actions are deliberate.

4. **Review live-operation risks.** Inspect current storefront access, providers, automatic fulfillment, Printify and other routing, digital delivery, email/SMS, Shopify Flow, inventory, fraud, label, tax, subscription, commission, webhook, and external integrations. Record what could trigger, what requires a controlled pause, who restores it, and how live customers might be affected. *Why it matters:* this helps prevent a test becoming a live event. *Done when:* safeguards and owners are recorded.

5. **Choose the payment-test method.** Choose Shopify Payments test mode, Bogus Gateway, a supported third-party test mode, a deliberately authorized real transaction followed by cancellation/refund, or Separate test required. These methods differ. Shopify Payments test mode currently requires completed setup and a paid plan; reverify both. Test mode can alter available wallet, express, local, or other methods and can affect live customers. Real transactions can incur fees that are not returned. Never put real card data in a simulated gateway or test credentials in a live field. [Shopify — Place a test order](https://help.shopify.com/en/manual/checkout-settings/test-orders) [Shopify — Use payment test mode](https://help.shopify.com/en/manual/checkout-settings/test-orders/payments-test-mode) [Shopify — Test Shopify Payments](https://help.shopify.com/en/manual/payments/shopify-payments/testing-shopify-payments) `[VERIFY CURRENT SHOPIFY SETTING]` *Why it matters:* this records what the selected route can represent. *Done when:* the route, limits, and live impact are documented.

6. **Record the pre-test state.** Record payment mode/providers, automatic fulfillment, provider apps, notifications, inventory, product status, channel availability, discount usage, shipping, tax display, storefront access, and time. Do not capture credentials. *Why it matters:* this creates the restoration reference. *Done when:* the baseline is complete.

All business names, personal names, customer information, addresses, email addresses, phone numbers, order numbers, payment details, test credentials, card numbers, CVVs, expiry values, domains, URLs, product identifiers, SKUs, inventory quantities, tax values, shipping rates, discount codes, policy text, provider details, app identifiers, analytics identifiers, fulfillment locations, tracking information, and private operational details must be fictional, blank, or fully redacted. Capture date: record it. Note: Shopify paths, checkout fields, payment test modes, credentials, shipping options, tax displays, notifications, order statuses, inventory behavior, fulfillment routing, policy links, app behavior, and deletion behavior can change. Never expose live payment credentials or reusable test-order access links.)*

One path does not prove every product is reachable. *Why it matters:* this tests the selected discovery path. *Done when:* the product is reached or failure recorded.

35. **Build the blocker register.** Record stage, scenario, expected/observed, severity, impacts, owner, action, retest, and status. Suggested Critical includes real charge/production, exposed data, or blocked live checkout; High includes checkout/shipping/price/product/routing/policy failures; Medium includes recoverable notification/mobile/inventory issues; Low is cosmetic. Severity requires merchant judgment. *Why it matters:* this prioritizes correction. *Done when:* every material issue has an owner and action.

`[INSERT SCREENSHOT — blocker register]` *(Locate: a fictional/redacted register. All business names, personal names, customer information, addresses, email addresses, phone numbers, order numbers, payment details, test credentials, card numbers, CVVs, expiry values, domains, URLs, product identifiers, SKUs, inventory quantities, tax values, shipping rates, discount codes, policy text, provider details, app identifiers, analytics identifiers, fulfillment locations, tracking information, and private operational details must be fictional, blank, or fully redacted. Capture date: record it. Note: Shopify paths, checkout fields, payment test modes, credentials, shipping options, tax displays, notifications, order statuses, inventory behavior, fulfillment routing, policy links, app behavior, and deletion behavior can change. Never expose live payment credentials or reusable test-order access links.)*

36. **Retest corrected blockers.** Reproduce original conditions, rerun the same path, record the new result and new issues, and update status. Say “retested successfully for this scenario,” never “permanently fixed.” *Why it matters:* this provides current evidence. *Done when:* corrected Critical/High items have evidence or remain open.

`[INSERT SCREENSHOT — retest evidence]` *(Locate: fictional/redacted retest evidence. All business names, personal names, customer information, addresses, email addresses, phone numbers, order numbers, payment details, test credentials, card numbers, CVVs, expiry values, domains, URLs, product identifiers, SKUs, inventory quantities, tax values, shipping rates, discount codes, policy text, provider details, app identifiers, analytics identifiers, fulfillment locations, tracking information, and private operational details must be fictional, blank, or fully redacted. Capture date: record it. Note: Shopify paths, checkout fields, payment test modes, credentials, shipping options, tax displays, notifications, order statuses, inventory behavior, fulfillment routing, policy links, app behavior, and deletion behavior can change. Never expose live payment credentials or reusable test-order access links.)*

37. **Make the Chapter 11 readiness decision.** Review open Critical/High blockers, complete and failed paths, notifications, safe routing, policies, desktop/mobile, restoration, model coverage, and tax/legal/accessibility/regional uncertainties. Choose Ready for Chapter 12, Ready with documented noncritical observations, or Not ready. This does not equal public launch. *Why it matters:* this informs the separate launch decision. *Done when:* the decision and evidence are recorded.

### Real example: Lucid Creations

Lucid Creations is the Shopify product-store branch under Auralis Digital and can include POD, handmade, and commission products; Printify is one POD route, while RJ Print is a separate 3D-printing branch. Its mixed fulfillment paths require separate routing evidence. Do not assume its current payment, shipping, tax, inventory, notification, policy, app, provider, or checkout configuration; place no real order, trigger no production, buy no label, expose no private information, and invent no result.

`[INSERT LUCID CREATIONS FULL CUSTOMER JOURNEY TEST EXAMPLE]` — selected product/variant; controlled test method; live/private state; provider and automation state; Printify, handmade, commission, and RJ Print routing; destinations; discount; tax presentation; policies; notifications; order/inventory effects; desktop/mobile and failure-path observations; blockers; restoration; and redaction needs.

### Common mistake

Running one successful checkout and treating it as proof the whole store is ready. That can miss failed payments, invalid addresses, absent shipping, variant or mobile problems, mixed fulfillment, broken policies, notification errors, provider triggers, inventory mismatches, test mode left active, and regional or method differences. Test the highest-risk paths, record exact scenarios, restore settings, and retest material corrections.

### Reality check

A successful test order is evidence for one tested scenario. It does not establish that every product, payment method, destination, device, app, fulfillment route, or customer journey will behave the same way.

### Checklist

- [ ] I listed every applicable customer journey.
- [ ] I defined pass, blocker, and observation.
- [ ] I created safe test data.
- [ ] I reviewed live-operation risks.
- [ ] I selected a test-payment method.
- [ ] I recorded the pre-test state.
- [ ] I tested the storefront entry point.
- [ ] I followed the navigation path to a product.
- [ ] I reviewed the collection page.
- [ ] I reviewed the product page.
- [ ] I tested representative variants.
- [ ] I reviewed the cart.
- [ ] I tested existing discounts where applicable.
- [ ] I reviewed checkout contact and address fields.
- [ ] I reviewed delivery and shipping options.
- [ ] I recorded tax presentation without treating it as tax advice.
- [ ] I opened applicable policy links.
- [ ] I ran a successful simulated-payment path where supported.
- [ ] I reviewed a failed or incomplete payment path where supported.
- [ ] I kept any deliberately authorized real-payment test separate from simulated testing.
- [ ] I reviewed the confirmation page.
- [ ] I reviewed customer notifications using Triggered / Not triggered / Unsupported classifications.
- [ ] I reviewed staff notifications using Triggered / Not triggered / Unsupported classifications.
- [ ] I reviewed the Shopify order record.
- [ ] I reviewed inventory effects.
- [ ] I reviewed fulfillment assignment without triggering real fulfillment.
- [ ] I reviewed each applicable fulfillment model.
- [ ] I reviewed cancellation/refund behavior safely where applicable.
- [ ] I did not assume a refund returns transaction or processing fees.
- [ ] I did not assume every test order can be deleted.
- [ ] I reviewed order-status access.
- [ ] I tested desktop and mobile separately.
- [ ] I completed an accessibility-oriented interaction review.
- [ ] I tested a relevant mixed or high-risk cart where applicable.
- [ ] I tested an incomplete customer journey.
- [ ] I restored temporary test settings.
- [ ] I confirmed payment test mode was returned to the intended state.
- [ ] I reviewed test artifacts.
- [ ] I created a prioritized blocker register.
- [ ] I retested corrected critical and high blockers.
- [ ] I made a Chapter 11 readiness decision.
- [ ] Launch remains deferred to Chapter 12.

### Before you continue
- Did I complete this step?
- Did I safely test the highest-risk customer journeys?
- Are all critical and high blockers resolved or clearly recorded?

- Main unresolved customer-journey blocker: _____________________________________

<!-- PAGE BREAK -->

## Chapter 12 — Launch, Review Basic Results, and Get Help if Needed

This is the launch chapter, and several steps are consequential: making the store public, removing the storefront password, and connecting a domain all change what real customers can see and do. Work through the stop-and-verify notes, then review calmly after launch.

### What you will complete

In this final chapter you turn your tested store into a deliberate public launch and a calm first review. You will revisit the Chapter 11 readiness decision, define exactly what is being launched, record the pre-launch state, and confirm the intended payment, fulfillment, inventory, shipping, policy, contact, product-publication, and domain state. You will make an explicit go/no-go decision, make the storefront public only when you are deliberately ready, verify the public result from a clean session, prepare a first-live-order workflow, review the first real order safely if one exists, record a basic analytics baseline, interpret early results cautiously, choose one bounded next improvement, and decide whether to continue on your own or request Shopify / E-commerce Setup help from Auralis Digital. You finish by completing the Final Launch Checklist.

### Why this matters

Launch is a deliberate action, not a reflex. Making the storefront public exposes whatever state the store is actually in — including anything left unfinished. A calm, recorded launch helps you identify immediate problems, establish a starting baseline, and route unresolved work to the right place. It does not, by itself, create traffic, sales, profit, trust, or long-term success. Treating launch as a checkpoint rather than a finish line is what keeps the first live orders manageable.

### Launch-safety boundary

This chapter describes how a merchant can launch a store. It does not launch any real store, and following it does not remove a real password, change a real plan, activate or deactivate a real payment provider, change test mode, connect or edit a real domain, modify DNS, publish or unpublish real products, place or fulfill a real order, trigger Printify or RJ Print production, buy a shipping label, send customer notifications, or change real analytics.

Before you make the storefront public, make a deliberate launch decision. Identify whether the store is already public, whether the current domain is usable, whether payment test mode is off, whether an active payment method can accept live payments, whether automatic fulfillment could trigger, whether products are published to the intended channel, whether inventory is available or intentionally configured, whether shipping or delivery methods are available for intended customers, whether policies and contact routes are public, whether critical or high Chapter 11 blockers remain, who will monitor the store immediately after launch, and what action will be taken if a critical problem appears. Removing password protection is not a routine button click — it makes the storefront publicly accessible. Do not disable a payment provider, fulfillment app, market, channel, or automation without recording the operational effect.

If a critical problem appears after launch, assess — rather than assume a single universal response — whether to restore password protection, pause an affected product or channel, stop fulfillment, contact Shopify or the relevant provider, or record and correct the issue.

### Step-by-step instructions

1. **Review the Chapter 11 decision.** Return to the Pre-Launch Store Test worksheet and record the Chapter 11 decision, open critical blockers, open high blockers, open medium blockers, deferred scenarios, untested payment methods, untested fulfillment models, unresolved tax questions, unresolved legal questions, unresolved accessibility concerns, and the temporary-settings restoration status. Do not automatically proceed simply because a successful test order exists. *Why it matters:* this establishes whether the store has sufficient evidence to enter a launch decision. *Done when:* the Chapter 11 result and remaining blockers are recorded.

`[INSERT SCREENSHOT — Chapter 11 readiness decision]` *(Locate: the recorded Chapter 11 readiness decision. All business names, store names, personal names, customer information, physical addresses, return addresses, email addresses, phone numbers, domains, internal admin URLs, preview links, checkout links, account identifiers, staff identities, order numbers, customer names, payment details, provider details, bank or payout information, test credentials, live credentials, domain verification values, DNS records, product identifiers, product names, prices, costs, SKUs, inventory quantities, shipping rates, tax values, discount codes, policy text, analytics values, session data, order data, sales data, traffic sources, fraud indicators, app identifiers, provider identifiers, fulfillment locations, tracking information, and private operational information must be fictional, blank, or fully redacted. Capture date: record it. Note: Shopify paths, store access and password controls, plan requirements, payment modes, domains, DNS and TLS status, product and channel availability, markets, shipping options, tax displays, policies, analytics metrics, analytics definitions, data delays, fraud analysis, order statuses, fulfillment routing, notifications, applications, redirect behavior, and support routes can change. Never expose live credentials, reusable checkout or order-status links, domain verification secrets, admin URLs, customer information, or real payment information.)*

2. **Define the launch scope.** Record what is being launched — the Online Store, specific products, specific collections, the domestic market, international markets, pickup, local delivery, subscriptions, digital products, services, POD products, handmade products, commission products, RJ Print products, customer accounts, and any other sales channels — and mark each one Included now, Already live, Deferred, Blocked, or Not applicable. Launching the Online Store does not automatically launch every market, sales channel, product, or app. *Why it matters:* this prevents "launch" from becoming an undefined all-platform action. *Done when:* the intended public scope is written.

3. **Choose the launch owner and observation window.** Record the launch owner, a backup contact, the planned launch date and time, the time zone, the immediate monitoring period, the first results review date, the first-order owner, the fulfillment owner, and the technical escalation route. Do not assume a universally ideal launch day or time. *Why it matters:* this establishes responsibility for the outward action and the immediate follow-up. *Done when:* ownership and timing are recorded.

4. **Record the pre-launch state.** Record the storefront password/access state, the current primary domain, other connected domains, the payment mode and provider, automatic fulfillment, provider apps, digital delivery, product publication, channel availability, inventory, shipping and delivery, tax presentation, policies, the contact route, notifications, discounts, customer accounts, analytics and tracking, open blockers, and the date and time. Do not record credentials or private admin links. *Why it matters:* this creates a reference for launch verification and rollback decisions. *Done when:* the baseline is documented.

`[INSERT SCREENSHOT — pre-launch state record]` *(Locate: the recorded pre-launch state. All business names, store names, personal names, customer information, physical addresses, return addresses, email addresses, phone numbers, domains, internal admin URLs, preview links, checkout links, account identifiers, staff identities, order numbers, customer names, payment details, provider details, bank or payout information, test credentials, live credentials, domain verification values, DNS records, product identifiers, product names, prices, costs, SKUs, inventory quantities, shipping rates, tax values, discount codes, policy text, analytics values, session data, order data, sales data, traffic sources, fraud indicators, app identifiers, provider identifiers, fulfillment locations, tracking information, and private operational information must be fictional, blank, or fully redacted. Capture date: record it. Note: Shopify paths, store access and password controls, plan requirements, payment modes, domains, DNS and TLS status, product and channel availability, markets, shipping options, tax displays, policies, analytics metrics, analytics definitions, data delays, fraud analysis, order statuses, fulfillment routing, notifications, applications, redirect behavior, and support routes can change. Never expose live credentials, reusable checkout or order-status links, domain verification secrets, admin URLs, customer information, or real payment information.)*

5. **Confirm account, plan, and administrative ownership.** Review the store owner, staff access, the billing contact, the plan state, payment-provider eligibility, two-step authentication, domain ownership, provider-app ownership, and renewal responsibility. Do not publish plan prices, trial dates, billing amounts, bank information, identity documents, recovery codes, or account emails. [Shopify — Choose a plan](https://help.shopify.com/en/manual/intro-to-shopify/pricing-plans/plans-features/choosing-a-plan) [Shopify — Review account security](https://help.shopify.com/en/manual/privacy-and-security/account-security/account-security-best-practices) `[VERIFY CURRENT SHOPIFY SETTING]` *Why it matters:* this helps identify account or billing restrictions that could block launch or operations. *Done when:* ownership and any unresolved restrictions are recorded.

6. **Confirm public business and contact information.** Review the public business identity, the Contact page, the support route, the customer-facing sender identity, the public email, a public phone if used, a public address if used, footer information, the About page, and response expectations, and confirm that no residential address or private account information is exposed unintentionally. Do not invent contact information or response times. *Why it matters:* this records how a live customer can identify and contact the business. *Done when:* the public information is accurate or the store remains blocked.

Printify remains one POD route; RJ Print remains a separate 3D-printing branch and must not be treated as Printify. Do not trigger production. *Why it matters:* this records where the first live order would go. *Done when:* every included product type has an operational route.

11. **Confirm the domain strategy.** A custom domain is optional; the Shopify-provided domain can still function as the store address and is a valid launch route when no custom domain is connected. Connecting a custom domain later involves DNS, the setting that points your web address to your store, which this guide treats as later technical work. Where used, a custom domain may be Shopify-managed, a third-party domain connected from an external provider, or a deliberately chosen subdomain, and it must be connected before it can be used as the intended primary domain. Record the intended customer-facing domain, connection status, ownership, renewal responsibility, TLS/security status where displayed, the primary-domain decision, redirect-domain behavior, and any unresolved DNS issue; domain and DNS changes can take time to propagate. Do not state that a custom domain is required, and do not expose DNS verification values or provider credentials in screenshots. [Shopify — Domains overview](https://help.shopify.com/en/manual/domains) [Shopify — Add or connect a domain](https://help.shopify.com/en/manual/domains/add-a-domain) [Shopify — Manage the primary domain](https://help.shopify.com/en/manual/domains/domain-type) `[VERIFY CURRENT SHOPIFY SETTING]` *Why it matters:* this records the address customers are intended to use. *Done when:* the intended domain works or launch remains blocked.

   **Stop and verify:** changing a domain or DNS settings can affect public access and can take time to propagate. Confirm the change before applying it.

14. **Perform a final accessibility-oriented review.** This is an accessibility-oriented review, not a formal accessibility audit or certification. Review keyboard access, focus, labels, errors, link names, button names, heading order, alt text, contrast concerns, zoom, tap targets, mobile interaction, and screen-reader behavior where available. *Why it matters:* this helps identify obvious interaction barriers before public access. *Done when:* material barriers are corrected or recorded.

15. **Perform a public-information and privacy review.** Search the storefront for residential addresses, personal phone numbers, private emails, internal admin URLs, provider account identifiers, draft policy text, test customer information, test orders, placeholder content, private notes, hidden credentials, internal product data, analytics identifiers displayed publicly, and unintended author or file metadata where inspectable. *Why it matters:* launch makes customer-facing content externally accessible. *Done when:* no known private or placeholder information remains public.

16. **Make the final go/no-go decision.** Answer each of the following before deciding: are critical blockers open; are high blockers open; is the payment live state confirmed; is test mode restored; is the domain usable; are products published as intended; is shipping or delivery available; are policies accessible; is the contact route accessible; is fulfillment routing ready; is inventory ready; was desktop checked; was mobile checked; was the public-data review completed; and is the launch owner available. Then record the decision as Go, No-go, Already live and continuing to post-launch verification, or Limited launch with documented exclusions. A "Go" decision does not equate to guaranteed success. *Why it matters:* this is the explicit authorization point before public access. *Done when:* the decision and its supporting evidence are recorded.

17. **Make the storefront public.** Only after a Go decision, confirm that payment test mode is in the intended final state, confirm the intended domain, confirm the public launch scope, remove storefront password protection where applicable, and record the action, date, time, owner, and result. Removing password protection makes the storefront publicly accessible. If the store is already public, do not toggle password protection merely to follow the guide. [Shopify — Prepare for launch](https://help.shopify.com/en/manual/intro-to-shopify/initial-setup/setup-prepare-for-launch) [Shopify — Manage storefront password protection](https://help.shopify.com/en/manual/online-store/themes/password-page) `[VERIFY CURRENT SHOPIFY SETTING]` *Why it matters:* this is the public launch action. *Done when:* public access is confirmed or the launch attempt is recorded as blocked.

   **Stop and verify:** making the storefront public exposes the store to real customers and affects public access. Confirm the store is genuinely ready before continuing.

19. **Run a public storefront smoke test.** Without placing an unnecessary real order, open the homepage, navigate to a collection, open a product, select a variant, add it to the cart, review the cart, enter checkout only as far as safely needed, review the shipping, contact, and policy presentation, and exit without triggering a real transaction unless it is separately authorized. Do not duplicate the full Chapter 11 test. *Why it matters:* this identifies whether launch changed the public customer path. *Done when:* the main public path works or the issue is recorded.

21. **Review immediate system behavior.** Observe error notices, payment-provider notices, domain warnings, app errors, provider-app activity, unexpected fulfillment triggers, unexpected emails or SMS, inventory changes, analytics activity, customer-account behavior, and channel warnings. Do not assume silence means every integration is working. *Why it matters:* this records immediate downstream effects of launch. *Done when:* unexpected behavior is recorded and assigned.

22. **Prepare the first-live-order workflow.** Before a real order arrives, record who reviews payment, who reviews fraud/risk indicators, who checks customer contact details, who verifies the product and variant, who checks inventory, who approves Printify or provider production, who handles handmade or RJ Print production, who buys a label, who sends customer communication, who handles cancellation and refund requests, and who records the order issue. Fraud analysis does not guarantee safety. *Why it matters:* this establishes ownership before customer money and fulfillment are involved. *Done when:* the first-order workflow has an owner for every applicable action.

23. **Review the first live order safely.** When a live order actually exists, review the payment status, fraud/risk information where available, customer contact information, shipping address, product, variant, quantity, price, discount, shipping, tax presentation, inventory, fulfillment assignment, provider trigger, timeline, and customer notification. Do not fulfill solely because payment appears successful, and do not invent a first order if none exists; valid states include no live order yet, live order reviewed, live order held for review, live order cancelled or refunded, and separate provider review required. [Shopify — Manage orders](https://help.shopify.com/en/manual/fulfillment/managing-orders) [Shopify — Review fraud analysis](https://help.shopify.com/en/manual/fulfillment/managing-orders/protecting-orders/fraud-analysis) `[VERIFY CURRENT SHOPIFY SETTING]` *Why it matters:* this applies the launch workflow to the first real operational event. *Done when:* the first order is safely handled or marked not yet available.

`[INSERT SCREENSHOT — first-live-order review or no-order-yet state]` *(Locate: the redacted first-live-order review, or the recorded "no live order yet" state. 24. **Record the launch analytics baseline.** Review available basic information such as sessions, visitors, orders, sales, conversion-related metrics, traffic source, device, landing page, product activity, checkout activity, abandoned checkout, and fulfillment status; not every metric is required. Record the date range, the data source, whether test traffic remains, whether merchant visits are included, whether the data appears delayed, whether third-party analytics disagrees, and which metrics are unavailable. Analytics can be delayed, and analytics tools can disagree because they count activity differently. [Shopify — View analytics](https://help.shopify.com/en/manual/reports-and-analytics/shopify-reports/overview-dashboard) [Shopify — Use Live View](https://help.shopify.com/en/manual/reports-and-analytics/shopify-reports/live-view) [Shopify — Understand data discrepancies](https://help.shopify.com/en/manual/reports-and-analytics/discrepancies) `[VERIFY CURRENT SHOPIFY SETTING]` *Why it matters:* this establishes a documented starting point. *Done when:* the selected baseline is recorded with its limitations.

All business names, store names, personal names, customer information, physical addresses, return addresses, email addresses, phone numbers, domains, internal admin URLs, preview links, checkout links, account identifiers, staff identities, order numbers, customer names, payment details, provider details, bank or payout information, test credentials, live credentials, domain verification values, DNS records, product identifiers, product names, prices, costs, SKUs, inventory quantities, shipping rates, tax values, discount codes, policy text, analytics values, session data, order data, sales data, traffic sources, fraud indicators, app identifiers, provider identifiers, fulfillment locations, tracking information, and private operational information must be fictional, blank, or fully redacted.
